        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer39815432" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1305177964448464747">I've always been a big fan of "whodunits" and of course you know of my love of historical novels, so I was pretty excited when I saw this book come out and immediately had to snatch it up. Summerscale writes a great novel of a murder mystery set it gothic Victorian London, where the family are the only suspects. The case proves to be very captivating with various theories laid out for the reader to examine. The author is very good at making it not feel like you're reading a non-fiction book that</span>
  <span id="freeText1305177964448464747" style="display:none">I've always been a big fan of "whodunits" and of course you know of my love of historical novels, so I was pretty excited when I saw this book come out and immediately had to snatch it up. Summerscale writes a great novel of a murder mystery set it gothic Victorian London, where the family are the only suspects. The case proves to be very captivating with various theories laid out for the reader to examine. The author is very good at making it not feel like you're reading a non-fiction book that just is crammed with facts, even though she has obviously done her research and has her share of documents pertaining to the case. I could've maybe done without the deeper information she gives into Whicher's life and the history of detective work, although some of it was insightful. Recommended to anyone who likes a good, real-life murder mystery!<br /></span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780747596486" href="/book/show/3891793-the-suspicions-of-mr-whicher">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6237864-nandakishore-varma?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/6237864-nandakishore-varma?shelf=history">history</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer232288878" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer283727550274528131">I would have given this four stars, but I had an issue with the way the book was structured. The author seemed to be not clear about what she was writing-a historical mystery, social commentary about Nineteenth Century England, or an exploration of the evolution of the fictional detective. The narration constantly switched between these modes and grated on the nerves at times. <br/><br/>That said, the mystery is excellent (with genuine clues, red herrings and all): and Inspector Whicher is as enthralling </span>
  <span id="freeText283727550274528131" style="display:none">I would have given this four stars, but I had an issue with the way the book was structured. The author seemed to be not clear about what she was writing-a historical mystery, social commentary about Nineteenth Century England, or an exploration of the evolution of the fictional detective. The narration constantly switched between these modes and grated on the nerves at times. <br /><br />That said, the mystery is excellent (with genuine clues, red herrings and all): and Inspector Whicher is as enthralling as any fictional detective, especially with regard to the one vital deduction which points to the solution of the mystery. <br /><br />I wish the author had structured the book differently, first giving us the mystery without any dressings and then analysing its social and literary impact. I feel it would have been more effective. <br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="283727550274528131" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_2377739050" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2377739050" />
    <a title="Jeimy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/9310118-jeimy"><img alt="Jeimy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1598145436p2/9310118.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2377739050?book_show_action=true">May 01, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jeimy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jeimy" href="/user/show/9310118-jeimy">Jeimy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2377739050" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5786807878496918702">This is a riveting work of narrative nonfiction that centers on the murder of an infant, the detective tasked to solve it, and the aftermath of his investigation--which led to his downfall. However, the book is much more than that. It offers a glimpse of the mythical status of detectives both in literature and in real life. You see, the rank of detective was created during the Victorian era and much of the public's fascination with them stemmed from the detective novel, a new genre at the time. </span>
  <span id="freeText5786807878496918702" style="display:none">This is a riveting work of narrative nonfiction that centers on the murder of an infant, the detective tasked to solve it, and the aftermath of his investigation--which led to his downfall. However, the book is much more than that. It offers a glimpse of the mythical status of detectives both in literature and in real life. You see, the rank of detective was created during the Victorian era and much of the public's fascination with them stemmed from the detective novel, a new genre at the time. The book also delves into the fascination that everyday Victorian citizens had with the goings on inside the manors, mansions, and castles of its wealthier citizens.</span>
  <a data-text-id="5786807878496918702" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>


      <div class="reviewFooter uitext buttons">
          

<tr class="no_border feedFooterReview" id="update_comment_stuff_Review2377739050">

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer223457679" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13407346228751524419">This is a strange book.....a mix of true crime, the rise of the detective and detective fiction of the mid-19th century. And it moves from one aspect of that mix to another with abandon.<br/>The horrific murder of a child (called the Road Murder)brings Mr. Whicher, one of the first detectives of Scotland Yard onto the scene. Although he identifies the murderer in a short time, the public refuses to accept his conclusions and he suffers public scorn. Running through the narrative are references to the</span>
  <span id="freeText13407346228751524419" style="display:none">This is a strange book.....a mix of true crime, the rise of the detective and detective fiction of the mid-19th century. And it moves from one aspect of that mix to another with abandon.<br />The horrific murder of a child (called the Road Murder)brings Mr. Whicher, one of the first detectives of Scotland Yard onto the scene. Although he identifies the murderer in a short time, the public refuses to accept his conclusions and he suffers public scorn. Running through the narrative are references to the rise of the detective story, especially those books by Wilkie Collins (The Woman in White, and The Moonstone). After several years, the murderer confesses and is sent to prison. Mr. Whicher regains his reputation and comes out of retirement.<br />It is a rather dry book with an unusual narrative style but the research is impeccable and it held my interest.</span>

<div id="review_396057722" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/396057722" />
    <a title="Lisa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1512868-lisa"><img alt="Lisa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1269709619p2/1512868.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/396057722?book_show_action=true">Feb 23, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lisa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lisa" href="/user/show/1512868-lisa">Lisa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=2017">2017</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=murders-and-misdeeds">murders-and-misdeeds</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=non-fic">non-fic</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=family-business">family-business</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=own">own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=kindle-baby">kindle-baby</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=history">history</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=social-commentary">social-commentary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1512868-lisa?shelf=true-crime">true-crime</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer396057722" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18216632283552163445">Do you enjoy Victorian literature? Fascinated by true crime? Then you could find a lot to enjoy in <i>The Suspicions of Mr Whicher</i>, which deals with a murder that rocked Victorian England – the death of the three year old darling of a middle class family, stolen from his crib in a locked house and found dumped in a privy with his throat cut.<br/><br/>Calling upon the Government to send one of its newly created detectives to solve the case (and receiving the titular Mr Whicher, one of their best), at first th</span>
  <span id="freeText18216632283552163445" style="display:none">Do you enjoy Victorian literature? Fascinated by true crime? Then you could find a lot to enjoy in <i>The Suspicions of Mr Whicher</i>, which deals with a murder that rocked Victorian England – the death of the three year old darling of a middle class family, stolen from his crib in a locked house and found dumped in a privy with his throat cut.<br /><br />Calling upon the Government to send one of its newly created detectives to solve the case (and receiving the titular Mr Whicher, one of their best), at first the public would be as fascinated by the detectives and the new ‘science’ of investigation as by the case and a wave of new novels starring fictional versions of these men fed the public’s mania. However, as the case progressed the public soon found themselves disgusted by the investigation in which everyone in the family was suspect (instead of just the help, which would have better suited middle class sensibilities) and the secrets of their home life laid bare for all to see in the newspapers. The disgust wasn’t confined to the brutality of the murder, or the idea that someone just like them had committed it, but was propelled by the investigation riding roughshod over the notion that an Englishman’s home was his castle, a bastion of middle class privacy that working class detectives had no right to intrude upon, and would leave Mr Whicher’s reputation in tatters.<br /><br />It was fascinating to see how what we take for granted in modern murder investigations was born, with hunches and interviews providing much of the ‘evidence’, and how Victorian manners could waylay cases (such as the local police not wishing to even look upon a ladies shift that could provide vital evidence, as such a thing was unseemly). The book laid out the facts of the case well, allowing the reader to follow the ‘clues’ in much the same was as Mr Whicher had, as well as providing fascinating commentary on how public opinion could affect both the outcome of case and his career.<br /><br />Informative and insightful, this book has also inspired me into a little mania of my own which has led to the addition roughly 10,000 more books to my wishlist. More of Summerscale’s books feature on the list, and pay day can’t come soon enough.<br /><br /><a href="https://cannonballread.com/2017/02/how-to-wind-up-your-betters/" rel="nofollow noopener">**Also posted at Cannonball Read 9**</a></span>
  <a data-text-id="18216632283552163445" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1653207566" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1653207566" />
    <a title="DeAnna Knippling" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3166570-deanna-knippling"><img alt="DeAnna Knippling" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1442526565p2/3166570.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1653207566?book_show_action=true">May 30, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="DeAnna Knippling" class="user" itemprop="url" name="DeAnna Knippling" href="/user/show/3166570-deanna-knippling">DeAnna Knippling</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3166570-deanna-knippling?shelf=nonfiction">nonfiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1653207566" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12742075027121067378">This book was 100% in my wheelhouse; I may not be able to be objective about it. <br/><br/>A well-researched book about a) a sensational crime in 1860 England, and b) how people reacted to the new detective force investigating it. It traces the change from half-thief thieftakers to the more staid, respectable detective force--and why. I kept throwing my hands in the air at the way people protested the detective doing his job (although it made perfect sense, from their closeted, secretive perspective). <br/><br/><a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(vi</a></span>
  <span id="freeText12742075027121067378" style="display:none">This book was 100% in my wheelhouse; I may not be able to be objective about it. <br /><br />A well-researched book about a) a sensational crime in 1860 England, and b) how people reacted to the new detective force investigating it. It traces the change from half-thief thieftakers to the more staid, respectable detective force--and why. I kept throwing my hands in the air at the way people protested the detective doing his job (although it made perfect sense, from their closeted, secretive perspective). <br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[<br />A few notes for me for later:<br /><br />--The murder was on the night of 29/30 June 1860.<br />--Jack/Jonathan Whicher born 1 Oct 1814, constable 18 Sep 1837<br />--Metropolitan (London) police founded 1829 by Sir Robert Peel<br />--Victoria crowned June 1838<br />--June 1842, requested permission to set up small detective division, Whicher was one of founding members<br />--Whicher made inspector in 1856<br />--25 April 1865, Constance starts process of confessing to being the sole perpetrator of the crime<br />--Victoria's mother died March 1861, Albert December 1861, she never came out of mourning<br />--Whicher leaves Metropolitan police 18 March 1864<br />--Constance moved from Salisbury to Milbank, Third Pentagon, 1866<br />--Whicher starts working as a private investigator early 1867<br />--Someone from Australia claiming to be Sir Roger Tichborne appears as claimant at end of 1866 (body lost in 1854)<br />--Whicher hired by Lord Arundel of Windsor, a relative, to unmask the Claimant, 1866 or 7? and worked on case for next SEVEN years, discovers him to be Arthur Orton, a butcher from Melbourne<br />--Which dies of gastritis/stomach ulcer, 29 June 1881<br />--Constance moved to Parkhurst (Isle of Wight), to Woking in Surrey, back to Milbank.<br />--After multiple failed petitions for release, let out on 20 year sentence, 18 July 1885.<br /> <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>
  <a data-text-id="12742075027121067378" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('tuR4vVVBn+PRbe2ktdOpHtbDMLFVPm/nASofnVtGcC3sZW1lZUM1NccSo3TBpjJMlWUnIzlFpH0flojDmnjd0g==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {
    new Ajax.Updater('addGroupBooks' + book_id + '', '/group/add_book_box', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){refreshGroupBoxComplete(request, book_id);}, parameters:'id=' + group_id + '&book_id=' + book_id + '&refresh=true' + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('FoUgJDTy+8XvzQNVOF1pLNF9qqKTd/Xtg9FfzIzU0RRMBDX8BPBRE/myTYVMKPJ+ktu9MP8MPnedbciSTep86w==')})
  }

After losing her job when the Garmin factory shuts down, Nonie is left with major money issues—as in, she has none. Desperate, she has to turn to the one place she’s avoided working since dropping out of college--her family’s funeral home.It wasn’t that Nonie feared the dead. In fact the opposite was true. She sees them—literally, and had been seeing the dead ever since she can remember. She tried telling her parents about it when she was a child, but they’d vehemently dismissed it to imaginary friends, so she’d stopped talking about it. There were only two people who actually knew Nonie’s secret and knew she was the real deal. One was Buggy Mouton, her best friend since kindergarten, and the other was Guy Skinard, whom Nonie considered her one true love since the day she’d laid eyes on him in high school. Buggy now worked at MeeMaw’s Café off of Main in Clay Point, and Guy, as usual, remained ever at her side—the only difference being he’d died in a freak boating accident nine years ago. Still the prankster even in death, Guy loved causing chaos, especially with men Nonie occasionally dated. She could only imagine what mayhem he might stir up while mirroring her at the funeral home and mingling with the newly departed.Only hours into the first day of her new job, pandemonium engulfs the funeral home, and much to Nonie’s surprise, it has nothing to do with Guy. It’s brought on by the family they’re serving. Seventy-two–year-old Dover Fontenot, the Major of Clay Point, dies in a car accident after indulging in a little extra-curricular activity—only the activity wasn’t with his wife , Hazel. And when Dover’s mistress, Anna Mae Turner, shows up at the funeral home for the visitation hell’s gates rip open and Broussard’s Funeral Home suddenly turns into a geriatric war zone.Amidst the flying hairpieces, canes and curses, Nonie’s best friend, Buggy, decides to drop by the funeral home with news she can’t wait to share. A cable network in New Orleans wants to put together a paranormal investigation team and have them check out purported haunted locations in Louisiana. For every location with solid filming potential, each member of the team gets a five hundred dollar pay check. And Buggy knows without question that if anyone can sniff out the ‘restless’ dead, it’s Nonie.Knowing nothing about paranormal investigation, but lured by the sound of the adventure and the dollar signs circling through her head, Nonie agrees to become part of the Boo Krewe, the name Buggy has formally given the makeshift team.The lure of money may be one thing, but what Nonie doesn’t realize is that occasionally the dead, so excited that someone can actually see and hear them, decide to follow her home, into her bed—the bathroom—the shower. And adding to this new-found frustration, Nonie barely finishes her first investigation when she gets a visit from Anna Mae Turner—the mayor’s former mistress—now dead, as well, and harassingly insistent that Nonie bring her killer to light.Nonie quickly learns that the ‘gift’ she’s carried since birth is really a giant wart on the butt of life—and no amount of Compound W is going to make it The appalling nature of the crimes—a brutal slaying in the gambling netherworld, the slaughter of two entire households, and the first of the modern lust-murders—was magnified not only by the lurid atmosphere of an age in which candlelight gave way to gaslight, but also by the efforts of some of the keenest minds of the period to uncover the gruesomest details of the killings.\n\nThese slayings all took place against the backdrop of a London in which the splendor of the fashionable world was haunted by the squalor of the slums. Sir Walter Scott, Lord Byron, Thomas De Quincey, Thomas Carlyle, and Percy Bysshe Shelley and others were fascinated by the blood and deviltry of these crimes.\n\nIn their contemplations of the most notorious murders of their time, they discerned in the act of killing itself a depth of hideousness that we have lost sight of, now living in an age in which murder has been reduced to a problem of social science and skillful detective work. Interweaving these cultural vignettes alongside criminal history, acclaimed author Michael Beran paints a vivid picture of a time when homicide was thought of as the intrusion of the diabolic into Detective fiction and the new police force developed in parallel, each imitating the other—the founders of Scotland Yard gave rise to Dickens\'s Inspector Bucket, the first fictional police detective, who in turn influenced Sherlock Holmes and, ultimately, even P.D. James and Patricia Cornwell.\n\nIn this meticulously researched and engrossing book, Judith Flanders retells the gruesome stories of many different types of murder, both famous and obscure: from in Great Britain.  With an irresistible cast of swindlers, forgers, and poisoners, the mad, the bad and the utterly dangerous, The Invention of Murder is both a mesmerizing tale of crime and       <div id="aboutAuthor" class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/author/show/11166.Kate_Summerscale">About Kate Summerscale</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class='bookAuthorProfile'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__topContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__photoContainer'>
<a href="/author/show/11166.Kate_Summerscale"><div class='bookAuthorProfile__photo' style='background-image: url(https://images.gr-assets.com/authors/1291700996p3/11166.jpg);'></div>
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__widgetContainer'>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__name'>
<a href="/author/show/11166.Kate_Summerscale">Kate Summerscale
</a></div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__followerCount'>
355
followers
</div>

</div>
</div>
<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer4463278247034434174">Kate Summerscale (born in 1965) is an English writer and journalist.<br/>She won the Samuel Johnson Prize for Non-fiction in 2008 with <i>The Suspicions of Mr Whicher or The Murder at Road Hill House</i> and won a Somerset Maugham Award in 1998 (and was shortlisted for the 1997 Whitbread Awards for biography) for the bestselling <i>The Queen of Whale Cay</i>, about Joe Carstairs, 'fastest woman on water'.<br/>As a journa</span>
  <span id="freeText4463278247034434174" style="display:none">Kate Summerscale (born in 1965) is an English writer and journalist.<br />She won the Samuel Johnson Prize for Non-fiction in 2008 with <i>The Suspicions of Mr Whicher or The Murder at Road Hill House</i> and won a Somerset Maugham Award in 1998 (and was shortlisted for the 1997 Whitbread Awards for biography) for the bestselling <i>The Queen of Whale Cay</i>, about Joe Carstairs, 'fastest woman on water'.<br />As a journalist, she worked for The Independent and The Daily Telegraph and her articles have appeared in The Guardian, The Daily Telegraph and The Sunday Telegraph. She stumbled on the story for <i> The Suspicions of Mr Whicher</i> in an 1890s anthology of unsolved crime stories and became so fascinated that she left her post as literary editor of <i>The Daily Telegraph</i> to pursue her investigations. She spent a year researching the book and another year writing it.<br />She has also judged various literary competitions including the Booker Prize in 2001.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4463278247034434174" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/trivia/work/3254095-the-suspicions-of-mr-whicher-murder-and-the-undoing-of-a-great-victori">Trivia About The Suspicions of...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
        <div class="mediumText">
          2 trivia questions
          <br />
          
        </div>
        <a class="actionLink" style="float: right;" href="/trivia/work/3254095-the-suspicions-of-mr-whicher-murder-and-the-undoing-of-a-great-victori">More quizzes &amp; trivia...</a>
<div class="clear"></div></div></div><div class="bigBoxBottom"></div></div>


      <div class=" clearFloats bigBox"><div class="h2Container gradientHeaderContainer"><h2 class="brownBackground"><a href="/work/quotes/3254095-the-suspicions-of-mr-whicher-murder-and-the-undoing-of-a-great-victori">Quotes from The Suspicions of...</a></h2></div><div class="bigBoxBody"><div class="bigBoxContent containerWithHeaderContent">
          <div class="stacked" tyle="padding-bottom:15px">
            <span class="readable">&ldquo;Perhaps this is the purpose of detective investigations, real and fictional -- to transform sensation, horror and grief into a puzzle, and then to solve the puzzle, to make it go away. 'The detective story,' observed Raymond Chandler in 1949, 'is a tragedy with a happy ending.' A storybook detective starts by confronting us with a murder and ends by absolving us of it. He clears us of guilt. He relieves us of uncertainty. He removes us from the presence of death.&rdquo;</span>
            <nobr>
              &mdash;
